<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<th>Supergroup affiliation</th>
<th>Members</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">Endosymbiont</a></th>
<th>Summary
</th></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primoplantae" title="Primoplantae">Primoplantae</a>/<br/><a href="/wiki/Archaeplastida" title="Archaeplastida">Archaeplastida</a>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">Chlorophyta</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhodophyta" title="Rhodophyta">Rhodophyta</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glaucophyta" title="Glaucophyta">Glaucophyta</a></li></ul>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Cyanobacteria" title="Cyanobacteria">Cyanobacteria</a>
</td>
<td>These algae have 'primary' <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast" title="Chloroplast">chloroplasts</a>, i.e. the chloroplasts are surrounded by two membranes and probably developed through a single endosymbiotic event. The chloroplasts of red algae have <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophylls</a> <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> (often), and <a href="/wiki/Phycobilin" title="Phycobilin">phycobilins</a>, while those of green algae have chloroplasts with chlorophyll <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> without phycobilins. Land plants are pigmented similarly to green algae and probably developed from them, thus the <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">Chlorophyta</a> is a sister taxon to the plants; sometimes the Chlorophyta, the <a href="/wiki/Charophyta" title="Charophyta">Charophyta</a>, and land plants are grouped together as the <a href="/wiki/Viridiplantae" title="Viridiplantae">Viridiplantae</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Excavata" title="Excavata">Excavata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhizaria" title="Rhizaria">Rhizaria</a>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorarachniophytes" title="Chlorarachniophytes">Chlorarachniophytes</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Euglenid" title="Euglenid">Euglenids</a></li></ul>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Green_algae" title="Green algae">Green algae</a>
</td>
<td>
<p>These groups have green chloroplasts containing chlorophylls <i>a</i> and <i>b</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BioRaven_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-BioRaven-17">[17]</a></sup> Their chloroplasts are surrounded by four and three membranes, respectively, and were probably retained from ingested green algae.
</p><p><b>Chlorarachniophytes</b>, which belong to the phylum <a href="/wiki/Cercozoa" title="Cercozoa">Cercozoa</a>, contain a small <a href="/wiki/Nucleomorph" title="Nucleomorph">nucleomorph</a>, which is a <a href="/wiki/Relict" title="Relict">relict</a> of the algae's <a href="/wiki/Cell_nucleus" title="Cell nucleus">nucleus</a>.
</p><p><b>Euglenids</b>, which belong to the phylum <a href="/wiki/Euglenozoa" title="Euglenozoa">Euglenozoa</a>, live primarily in fresh water and have chloroplasts with only three membranes. The endosymbiotic green algae may have been acquired through <a href="/wiki/Myzocytosis" title="Myzocytosis">myzocytosis</a> rather than <a href="/wiki/Phagocytosis" title="Phagocytosis">phagocytosis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Chromista" title="Chromista">Chromista</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolata" title="Alveolata">Alveolata</a>
</td>
<td>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Heterokont" title="Heterokont">Heterokonts</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haptophyta" title="Haptophyta">Haptophyta</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cryptomonad" title="Cryptomonad">Cryptomonads</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dinoflagellate" title="Dinoflagellate">Dinoflagellates</a></li></ul>
</td>
<td><a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">Red algae</a>
</td>
<td>
<p>These groups have chloroplasts containing chlorophylls <i>a</i> and <i>c</i>, and phycobilins. The shape varies from plant to plant; they may be of discoid, plate-like, reticulate, cup-shaped, spiral, or ribbon shaped. They have one or more pyrenoids to preserve protein and starch. The latter chlorophyll type is not known from any prokaryotes or primary chloroplasts, but genetic similarities with red algae suggest a relationship there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first three of these groups (Chromista), the chloroplast has four membranes, retaining a <a href="/wiki/Nucleomorph" title="Nucleomorph">nucleomorph</a> in <a href="/wiki/Cryptomonad" title="Cryptomonad">cryptomonads</a>, and they likely share a common pigmented ancestor, although other evidence casts doubt on whether the <a href="/wiki/Heterokont" title="Heterokont">heterokonts</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haptophyta" title="Haptophyta">Haptophyta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cryptomonad" title="Cryptomonad">cryptomonads</a> are in fact more closely related to each other than to other groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-parfrey_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-parfrey-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>The typical dinoflagellate chloroplast has three membranes, but considerable diversity exists in chloroplasts within the group, and a number of endosymbiotic events apparently occurred.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keeling_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-keeling-5">[5]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Apicomplexa" title="Apicomplexa">Apicomplexa</a>, a group of closely related parasites, also have plastids called <a href="/wiki/Apicoplast" title="Apicoplast">apicoplasts</a>, which are not photosynthetic, but appear to have a common origin with <a href="/wiki/Dinoflagellate" title="Dinoflagellate">dinoflagellate</a> chloroplasts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-keeling_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-keeling-5">[5]</a></sup>
</p>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_(Titelblatt).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1462" data-file-width="1005" height="247" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_%28Titelblatt%29.png/170px-Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_%28Titelblatt%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_%28Titelblatt%29.png/255px-Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_%28Titelblatt%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_%28Titelblatt%29.png/340px-Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_%28Titelblatt%29.png 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gmelin_-_Historia_Fucorum_(Titelblatt).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Title page of <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Gottlieb_Gmelin" title="Samuel Gottlieb Gmelin">Gmelin's</a> <i>Historia Fucorum</i>, dated 1768</div></div></div>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linnaeus" title="Linnaeus">Linnaeus</a>, in <i><a href="/wiki/Species_Plantarum" title="Species Plantarum">Species Plantarum</a></i> (1753),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> the starting point for modern <a href="/wiki/Botanical_nomenclature" title="Botanical nomenclature">botanical nomenclature</a>, recognized 14 genera of algae, of which only four are currently considered among algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> In <i><a href="/wiki/10th_edition_of_Systema_Naturae" title="10th edition of Systema Naturae">Systema Naturae</a></i>, Linnaeus described the genera <i><a href="/wiki/Volvox" title="Volvox">Volvox</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Corallina" title="Corallina">Corallina</a></i>, and a species of <i><a href="/wiki/Acetabularia" title="Acetabularia">Acetabularia</a></i> (as <i><a href="/wiki/Madrepora" title="Madrepora">Madrepora</a></i>), among the animals.
</p><p>In 1768, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Gottlieb_Gmelin" title="Samuel Gottlieb Gmelin">Samuel Gottlieb Gmelin</a> (1744–1774) published the <i>Historia Fucorum</i>, the first work dedicated to marine algae and the first book on <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">marine biology</a> to use the then new binomial nomenclature of Linnaeus. It included elaborate illustrations of seaweed and marine algae on folded leaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W.H.Harvey" title="W.H.Harvey">W.H.Harvey</a> (1811—1866) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lamouroux" title="Lamouroux">Lamouroux</a> (1813)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> were the first to divide macroscopic algae into four divisions based on their pigmentation. This is the first use of a biochemical criterion in plant systematics. Harvey's four divisions are: red algae (Rhodospermae), brown algae (Melanospermae), green algae (Chlorospermae), and Diatomaceae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dixon_73_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dixon_73-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>At this time, microscopic algae were discovered and reported by a different group of workers (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Otto_Friedrich_M%C3%BCller" title="Otto Friedrich Müller">O. F. Müller</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_Gottfried_Ehrenberg" title="Christian Gottfried Ehrenberg">Ehrenberg</a>) studying the <a href="/wiki/Infusoria" title="Infusoria">Infusoria</a> (microscopic organisms). Unlike <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macroalgae" title="Macroalgae">macroalgae</a>, which were clearly viewed as plants, <a href="/wiki/Microalgae" title="Microalgae">microalgae</a> were frequently considered animals because they are often motile.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> Even the nonmotile (coccoid) microalgae were sometimes merely seen as stages of the lifecycle of plants, macroalgae, or animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although used as a taxonomic category in some pre-Darwinian classifications, e.g., Linnaeus (1753), de Jussieu (1789), Horaninow (1843), Agassiz (1859), Wilson &amp; Cassin (1864), in further classifications, the "algae" are seen as an artificial, polyphyletic group.
</p><p>Throughout the 20th century, most classifications treated the following groups as divisions or classes of algae: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyanophyte" title="Cyanophyte">cyanophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhodophyte" title="Rhodophyte">rhodophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chrysophyte" title="Chrysophyte">chrysophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xanthophyte" title="Xanthophyte">xanthophytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diatom" title="Diatom">bacillariophytes</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phaeophyte" title="Phaeophyte">phaeophytes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dinoflagellate#history" title="Dinoflagellate">pyrrhophytes</a> (<a href="/wiki/Cryptomonad" title="Cryptomonad">cryptophytes</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinophyte" title="Dinophyte">dinophytes</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euglenophyte" title="Euglenophyte">euglenophytes</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chlorophyte" title="Chlorophyte">chlorophytes</a>. Later, many new groups were discovered (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Bolidophyceae" title="Bolidophyceae">Bolidophyceae</a>), and others were splintered from older groups: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charophyte" title="Charophyte">charophytes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glaucophyte" title="Glaucophyte">glaucophytes</a> (from chlorophytes), many <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterokontophyte" title="Heterokontophyte">heterokontophytes</a> (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Synurophyceae" title="Synurophyceae">synurophytes</a> from chrysophytes, or <a href="/wiki/Eustigmatophyte" title="Eustigmatophyte">eustigmatophytes</a> from xanthophytes), <a href="/wiki/Haptophyte" title="Haptophyte">haptophytes</a> (from chrysophytes), and <a href="/wiki/Chlorarachniophyte" title="Chlorarachniophyte">chlorarachniophytes</a> (from xanthophytes).
</p><p>With the abandonment of plant-animal dichotomous classification, most groups of algae (sometimes all) were included in <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">Protista</a>, later also abandoned in favour of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eukaryota" title="Eukaryota">Eukaryota</a>. However, as a legacy of the older plant life scheme, some groups that were also treated as <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">protozoans</a> in the past still have duplicated classifications (see <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ambiregnal_protist" title="Ambiregnal protist">ambiregnal protists</a>).
</p><p>Some parasitic algae (e.g., the green algae <i><a href="/wiki/Prototheca" title="Prototheca">Prototheca</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Helicosporidium" title="Helicosporidium">Helicosporidium</a></i>, parasites of metazoans, or <i><a href="/wiki/Cephaleuros" title="Cephaleuros">Cephaleuros</a></i>, parasites of plants) were originally classified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fungi" title="Fungi">fungi</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sporozoan" title="Sporozoan">sporozoans</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protistans</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Incertae_sedis" title="Incertae sedis">incertae sedis</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> while others (e.g., the green algae <i><a href="/wiki/Phyllosiphon" title="Phyllosiphon">Phyllosiphon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Rhodochytrium" title="Rhodochytrium">Rhodochytrium</a></i>, parasites of plants, or the red algae <i><a href="/wiki/Pterocladiophila" title="Pterocladiophila">Pterocladiophila</a></i> and <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Gelidiocolax_mammillatus&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Gelidiocolax mammillatus (page does not exist)">Gelidiocolax mammillatus</a></i>, parasites of other red algae, or the dinoflagellates <i><a href="/wiki/Oodinium" title="Oodinium">Oodinium</a></i>, parasites of fish) had their relationship with algae conjectured early. In other cases, some groups were originally characterized as parasitic algae (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Chlorochytrium" title="Chlorochytrium">Chlorochytrium</a></i>), but later were seen as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endophytic" title="Endophytic">endophytic</a> algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Some filamentous bacteria (e.g., <i><a href="/wiki/Beggiatoa" title="Beggiatoa">Beggiatoa</a></i>) were originally seen as algae. Furthermore, groups like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apicomplexan" title="Apicomplexan">apicomplexans</a> are also parasites derived from ancestors that possessed plastids, but are not included in any group traditionally seen as algae.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_to_land_plants">Relationship to land plants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Relationship to land plants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The first land plants probably evolved from shallow freshwater charophyte algae much like <i><a href="/wiki/Chara_(alga)" title="Chara (alga)">Chara</a></i> almost 500 million years ago. These probably had an isomorphic <a href="/wiki/Alternation_of_generations" title="Alternation of generations">alternation of generations</a> and were probably filamentous. Fossils of isolated land plant spores suggest land plants may have been around as long as 475 million years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wellman2003_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wellman2003-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Morphology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2301" data-file-width="1455" height="269" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg/170px-Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg/255px-Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg/340px-Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kelp-forest-Monterey.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Kelp_forest" title="Kelp forest">kelp forest</a> exhibit at the Monterey Bay Aquarium: A three-dimensional, multicellular thallus</div></div></div>
<p>A range of algal <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(biology)" title="Morphology (biology)">morphologies</a> is exhibited, and <a href="/wiki/Convergent_evolution" title="Convergent evolution">convergence</a> of features in unrelated groups is common. The only groups to exhibit three-dimensional multicellular <a href="/wiki/Thallus" title="Thallus">thalli</a> are the <a href="/wiki/Red_algae" title="Red algae">reds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Brown_algae" title="Brown algae">browns</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">chlorophytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xiao2004_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Xiao2004-36">[36]</a></sup> Apical growth is constrained to subsets of these groups: the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Florideophyceae" title="Florideophyceae">florideophyte</a> reds, various browns, and the charophytes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xiao2004_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Xiao2004-36">[36]</a></sup> The form of charophytes is quite different from those of reds and browns, because they have distinct nodes, separated by internode 'stems'; whorls of branches reminiscent of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Horsetail" title="Horsetail">horsetails</a> occur at the nodes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xiao2004_36-2"><a href="#cite_note-Xiao2004-36">[36]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Conceptacle" title="Conceptacle">Conceptacles</a> are another <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polyphyletic" title="Polyphyletic">polyphyletic</a> trait; they appear in the <a href="/wiki/Coralline_algae" title="Coralline algae">coralline algae</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Hildenbrandiales" title="Hildenbrandiales">Hildenbrandiales</a>, as well as the browns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Xiao2004_36-3"><a href="#cite_note-Xiao2004-36">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>Most of the simpler algae are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unicellular" title="Unicellular">unicellular</a> <a href="/wiki/Flagellate" title="Flagellate">flagellates</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Amoeboid" title="Amoeboid">amoeboids</a>, but colonial and nonmotile forms have developed independently among several of the groups. Some of the more common organizational levels, more than one of which may occur in the <a href="/wiki/Biological_life_cycle" title="Biological life cycle">lifecycle</a> of a species, are
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Colony_(biology)" title="Colony (biology)">Colonial</a>: small, regular groups of motile cells</li>
<li>Capsoid: individual non-motile cells embedded in <a href="/wiki/Mucilage" title="Mucilage">mucilage</a></li>
<li>Coccoid: individual non-motile cells with cell walls</li>
<li>Palmelloid: nonmotile cells embedded in mucilage</li>
<li>Filamentous: a string of nonmotile cells connected together, sometimes branching</li>
<li>Parenchymatous: cells forming a thallus with partial differentiation of tissues</li></ul>
<p>In three lines, even higher levels of organization have been reached, with full tissue differentiation. These are the brown algae,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>—some of which may reach 50 m in length (<a href="/wiki/Kelp" title="Kelp">kelps</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thomas_02_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas_02-38">[38]</a></sup>—the red algae,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> and the green algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> The most complex forms are found among the charophyte algae (see <a href="/wiki/Charales" title="Charales">Charales</a> and <a href="/wiki/Charophyta" title="Charophyta">Charophyta</a>), in a lineage that eventually led to the higher land plants. The innovation that defines these nonalgal plants is the presence of female reproductive organs with protective cell layers that protect the zygote and developing embryo. Hence, the land plants are referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Embryophyte" title="Embryophyte">Embryophytes</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physiology">Physiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Many algae, particularly members of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Characeae" title="Characeae">Characeae</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> have served as model experimental organisms to understand the mechanisms of the water permeability of membranes, <a href="/wiki/Osmoregulation" title="Osmoregulation">osmoregulation</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Turgor_regulation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Turgor regulation (page does not exist)">turgor regulation</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_tolerance" title="Salt tolerance">salt tolerance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cytoplasmic_streaming" title="Cytoplasmic streaming">cytoplasmic streaming</a>, and the generation of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Action_potentials" title="Action potentials">action potentials</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phytohormone" title="Phytohormone">Phytohormones</a> are found not only in higher plants, but in algae, too.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Symbiotic_algae">Symbiotic algae</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Symbiotic algae">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Some species of algae form <a href="/wiki/Symbiosis" title="Symbiosis">symbiotic relationships</a> with other organisms. In these symbioses, the algae supply photosynthates (organic substances) to the host organism providing protection to the algal cells. The host organism derives some or all of its energy requirements from the algae. Examples are:
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lichens">Lichens</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Lichens">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">Lichen</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_(stevefe).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1581" data-file-width="2047" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_%28stevefe%29.jpg/220px-Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_%28stevefe%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_%28stevefe%29.jpg/330px-Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_%28stevefe%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_%28stevefe%29.jpg/440px-Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_%28stevefe%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lichens_near_Clogher_Head_(stevefe).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Rock lichens in Ireland</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">Lichens</a> are defined by the <a href="/wiki/International_Association_for_Lichenology" title="International Association for Lichenology">International Association for Lichenology</a> to be "an association of a fungus and a photosynthetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Symbiont" title="Symbiont">symbiont</a> resulting in a stable vegetative body having a specific structure."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The fungi, or mycobionts, are mainly from the <a href="/wiki/Ascomycota" title="Ascomycota">Ascomycota</a> with a few from the <a href="/wiki/Basidiomycota" title="Basidiomycota">Basidiomycota</a>. In nature they do not occur separate from lichens. It is unknown when they began to associate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> One mycobiont associates with the same phycobiont species, rarely two, from the green algae, except that alternatively, the mycobiont may associate with a species of cyanobacteria (hence "photobiont" is the more accurate term). A photobiont may be associated with many different mycobionts or may live independently; accordingly, lichens are named and classified as fungal species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> The association is termed a morphogenesis because the lichen has a form and capabilities not possessed by the symbiont species alone (they can be experimentally isolated). The photobiont possibly triggers otherwise latent genes in the mycobiont.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Trentepohlia_(alga)" title="Trentepohlia (alga)">Trentepohlia</a> is an example of a common green alga genus worldwide that can grow on its own or be lichenised. Lichen thus share some of the habitat and often similar appearance with specialized species of algae (<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aerophyte" title="Aerophyte">aerophytes</a></i>) growing on exposed surfaces such as tree trunks and rocks and sometimes discoloring them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coral_reefs">Coral reefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Coral reefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">Coral</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">Coral reef</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Symbiodinium" title="Symbiodinium">Symbiodinium</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Reef.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2400" data-file-width="3593" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Coral_Reef.jpg/220px-Coral_Reef.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Coral_Reef.jpg/330px-Coral_Reef.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Coral_Reef.jpg/440px-Coral_Reef.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coral_Reef.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Floridian coral reef</div></div></div> <p><a href="/wiki/Coral_reef" title="Coral reef">Coral reefs</a> are accumulated from the <a href="/wiki/Calcareous" title="Calcareous">calcareous</a> <a href="/wiki/Exoskeleton" title="Exoskeleton">exoskeletons</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Marine_invertebrate" title="Marine invertebrate">marine invertebrates</a> of the order <a href="/wiki/Scleractinia" title="Scleractinia">Scleractinia</a> (stony <a href="/wiki/Coral" title="Coral">corals</a>). These <a href="/wiki/Animal#Food_and_energy_sourcing" title="Animal">animals</a> <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolize</a> <a href="/wiki/Sugar#Chemistry" title="Sugar">sugar</a> and oxygen to obtain energy for their cell-building processes, including <a href="/wiki/Secretion" title="Secretion">secretion</a> of the exoskeleton, with water and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> as byproducts. Dinoflagellates (algal protists) are often <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbionts</a> in the cells of the coral-forming marine invertebrates, where they accelerate host-cell metabolism by generating sugar and oxygen immediately available through photosynthesis using incident light and the carbon dioxide produced by the host. Reef-building stony corals (<a href="/wiki/Hermatypic_coral" title="Hermatypic coral">hermatypic corals</a>) require endosymbiotic algae from the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Symbiodinium" title="Symbiodinium">Symbiodinium</a></i> to be in a healthy condition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> The loss of <i>Symbiodinium</i> from the host is known as <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a>, a condition which leads to the deterioration of a reef.
</p><h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sea_sponges">Sea sponges</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Sea sponges">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sea_sponge" title="Sea sponge">Sea sponge</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">Endosymbiontic</a> green algae live close to the surface of some sponges, for example, breadcrumb sponges (<i><a href="/wiki/Halichondria_panicea" title="Halichondria panicea">Halichondria panicea</a></i>). The alga is thus protected from predators; the sponge is provided with oxygen and sugars which can account for 50 to 80% of sponge growth in some species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifecycle">Lifecycle</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Lifecycle">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhodophyta" title="Rhodophyta">Rhodophyta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyta" title="Chlorophyta">Chlorophyta</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heterokontophyta" title="Heterokontophyta">Heterokontophyta</a>, the three main algal <a href="/wiki/Phylum" title="Phylum">divisions</a>, have lifecycles which show considerable variation and complexity. In general, an asexual phase exists where the seaweed's cells are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diploid" title="Diploid">diploid</a>, a sexual phase where the cells are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haploid" title="Haploid">haploid</a>, followed by fusion of the male and female <a href="/wiki/Gamete" title="Gamete">gametes</a>. Asexual reproduction permits efficient population increases, but less variation is possible. Commonly, in sexual reproduction of unicellular and colonial algae, two specialized, sexually compatible, haploid gametes make physical contact and fuse to form a <a href="/wiki/Zygote" title="Zygote">zygote</a>. To ensure a successful mating, the development and release of gametes is highly synchronized and regulated; pheromones may play a key role in these processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid24597605_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid24597605-49">[49]</a></sup> Sexual reproduction allows for more variation and provides the benefit of efficient recombinational repair of DNA damages during meiosis, a key stage of the sexual cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> However, sexual reproduction is more costly than asexual reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid19441962_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid19441962-51">[51]</a></sup> Meiosis has been shown to occur in many different species of algae.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid773364_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid773364-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Conceptacle" title="Conceptacle">Conceptacle</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers">Numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2741" data-file-width="3908" height="154" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg/220px-Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg/330px-Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg/440px-Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Taiwan_2009_East_Coast_ShihTiPing_Giant_Stone_Steps_Algae_FRD_6581.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Algae on coastal rocks at <a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E7%9F%B3%E6%A2%AF%E5%9D%AA" title="zh:石梯坪">Shihtiping</a> in <a href="/wiki/Taiwan" title="Taiwan">Taiwan</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <i>Algal Collection of the US National Herbarium</i> (located in the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum of Natural History</a>) consists of approximately 320,500 dried specimens, which, although not exhaustive (no exhaustive collection exists), gives an idea of the order of magnitude of the number of algal species (that number remains unknown).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Estimates vary widely. For example, according to one standard textbook,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-John_02_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-John_02-54">[54]</a></sup> in the <a href="/wiki/British_Isles" title="British Isles">British Isles</a> the <i>UK Biodiversity Steering Group Report</i> estimated there to be 20,000 algal species in the UK. Another checklist reports only about 5,000 species. Regarding the difference of about 15,000 species, the text concludes: "It will require many detailed field surveys before it is possible to provide a reliable estimate of the total number of species ..."
</p><p>Regional and group estimates have been made, as well:
</p>
<ul><li>5,000–5,500 species of red algae worldwide</li>
<li>"some 1,300 in Australian Seas"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Huisman_00_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Huisman_00-55">[55]</a></sup></li>
<li>400 seaweed species for the western coastline of South Africa,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stegenga_97_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stegenga_97-56">[56]</a></sup> and 212 species from the coast of KwaZulu-Natal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Some of these are duplicates, as the range extends across both coasts, and the total recorded is probably about 500 species. Most of these are listed in <a href="/wiki/List_of_seaweeds_of_South_Africa" title="List of seaweeds of South Africa">List of seaweeds of South Africa</a>. These exclude <a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a> and crustose corallines.</li>
<li>669 marine species from California (US)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abbott_and_Hollenberg_76_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abbott_and_Hollenberg_76-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
<li>642 in the check-list of Britain and Ireland<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hardy_nad_Guiry_06_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hardy_nad_Guiry_06-59">[59]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>and so on, but lacking any scientific basis or reliable sources, these numbers have no more credibility than the British ones mentioned above. Most estimates also omit microscopic algae, such as phytoplankton.
</p><p>The most recent estimate suggests 72,500 algal species worldwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution">Distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Distribution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The distribution of algal species has been fairly well studied since the founding of <a href="/wiki/Phytogeography" title="Phytogeography">phytogeography</a> in the mid-19th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Round8_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Round8-61">[61]</a></sup> Algae spread mainly by the dispersal of <a href="/wiki/Spore" title="Spore">spores</a> analogously to the dispersal of Plantae by seeds and spores. This dispersal can be accomplished by air, water, or other organisms. Due to this, spores can be found in a variety of environments: fresh and marine waters, air, soil, and in or on other organisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Round8_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Round8-61">[61]</a></sup> Whether a spore is to grow into an organism depends on the combination of the species and the environmental conditions where the spore lands.
</p><p>The spores of freshwater algae are dispersed mainly by running water and wind, as well as by living carriers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Round8_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-Round8-61">[61]</a></sup> However, not all bodies of water can carry all species of algae, as the chemical composition of certain water bodies limits the algae that can survive within them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Round8_61-3"><a href="#cite_note-Round8-61">[61]</a></sup> Marine spores are often spread by ocean currents. Ocean water presents many vastly different habitats based on temperature and nutrient availability, resulting in phytogeographic zones, regions, and provinces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>To some degree, the distribution of algae is subject to floristic discontinuities caused by geographical features, such as <a href="/wiki/Antarctica" title="Antarctica">Antarctica</a>, long distances of ocean or general land masses. It is, therefore, possible to identify species occurring by locality, such as "Pacific algae" or "North Sea algae". When they occur out of their localities, hypothesizing a transport mechanism is usually possible, such as the hulls of ships. For example, <i>Ulva reticulata</i> and <i>U. fasciata</i> travelled from the mainland to Hawaii in this manner.
</p><p>Mapping is possible for select species only: "there are many valid examples of confined distribution patterns."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> For example, <i>Clathromorphum</i> is an arctic genus and is not mapped far south of there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> However, scientists regard the overall data as insufficient due to the "difficulties of undertaking such studies."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology">Ecology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Ecology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="512" data-file-width="640" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg/220px-Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg/330px-Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg/440px-Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Phytoplankton_Lake_Chuzenji.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Phytoplankton, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lake_Chuzenji" title="Lake Chuzenji">Lake Chuzenji</a></div></div></div>
<p>Algae are prominent in bodies of water, common in terrestrial environments, and are found in unusual environments, such as on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Snow_algae" title="Snow algae">snow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ice_algae" title="Ice algae">ice</a>. Seaweeds grow mostly in shallow marine waters, under 100 m (330 ft) deep; however, some such as <a href="/wiki/Navicula" title="Navicula">Navicula</a> pennata have been recorded to a depth of 360 m (1,180 ft).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>The various sorts of algae play significant roles in aquatic ecology. Microscopic forms that live suspended in the water column (<a href="/wiki/Phytoplankton" title="Phytoplankton">phytoplankton</a>) provide the food base for most marine <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chains</a>. In very high densities (<a href="/wiki/Algal_bloom" title="Algal bloom">algal blooms</a>), these algae may discolor the water and outcompete, poison, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asphyxiate" title="Asphyxiate">asphyxiate</a> other life forms.
</p><p>Algae can be used as <a href="/wiki/Indicator_organism" title="Indicator organism">indicator organisms</a> to monitor pollution in various aquatic systems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omar2010_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-Omar2010-67">[67]</a></sup> In many cases, algal metabolism is sensitive to various pollutants. Due to this, the species composition of algal populations may shift in the presence of chemical pollutants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omar2010_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-Omar2010-67">[67]</a></sup> To detect these changes, algae can be sampled from the environment and maintained in laboratories with relative ease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Omar2010_67-2"><a href="#cite_note-Omar2010-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>On the basis of their habitat, algae can be categorized as: <a href="/wiki/Aquatic_plant" title="Aquatic plant">aquatic</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Planktonic" title="Planktonic">planktonic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benthic" title="Benthic">benthic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marine_biology" title="Marine biology">marine</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lentic" title="Lentic">lentic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lotic" title="Lotic">lotic</a>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Terrestrial_plant" title="Terrestrial plant">terrestrial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerobiology" title="Aerobiology">aerial</a> (subareial),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lithophytic" title="Lithophytic">lithophytic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Halophytic" title="Halophytic">halophytic</a> (or <a href="/wiki/Euryhaline" title="Euryhaline">euryhaline</a>), <a href="/wiki/Psammon" title="Psammon">psammon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thermophilic" title="Thermophilic">thermophilic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychrophile" title="Psychrophile">cryophilic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Epibiont" title="Epibiont">epibiont</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epiphytic" title="Epiphytic">epiphytic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epizoic" title="Epizoic">epizoic</a>), <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiont</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endophytic" title="Endophytic">endophytic</a>, endozoic), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasitic" title="Parasitic">parasitic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Calcareous" title="Calcareous">calcifilic</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lichen" title="Lichen">lichenic</a> (phycobiont).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_associations">Cultural associations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Cultural associations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Classical_Chinese" title="Classical Chinese">classical Chinese</a>, the word <span lang="zh" title="Chinese language text"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%97%BB" title="wikt:藻">藻</a></span> is used both for "algae" and (in the modest tradition of the <a href="/wiki/Scholar-official" title="Scholar-official">imperial scholars</a>) for "literary talent". The third island in <a href="/wiki/Kunming_Lake" title="Kunming Lake">Kunming Lake</a> beside the <a href="/wiki/Summer_Palace" title="Summer Palace">Summer Palace</a> in Beijing is known as the Zaojian Tang Dao, which thus simultaneously means "Island of the Algae-Viewing Hall" and "Island of the Hall for Reflecting on Literary Talent".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Uses">Uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Algae_Harvester.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="375" data-file-width="500" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Algae_Harvester.jpg/220px-Algae_Harvester.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Algae_Harvester.jpg/330px-Algae_Harvester.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Algae_Harvester.jpg/440px-Algae_Harvester.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Algae_Harvester.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Harvesting algae</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agar">Agar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Agar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agar" title="Agar">Agar</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Gelatin" title="Gelatin">gelatinous</a> substance derived from red algae, has a number of commercial uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lewis_''et_al.''_88_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lewis_''et_al.''_88-71">[71]</a></sup> It is a good medium on which to grow bacteria and fungi, as most microorganisms cannot digest agar.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alginates">Alginates</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Alginates">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Alginic acid, or alginate, is extracted from brown algae. Its uses range from gelling agents in food, to medical dressings. Alginic acid also has been used in the field of <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> as a <a href="/wiki/Biocompatibility" title="Biocompatibility">biocompatible medium</a> for cell encapsulation and cell immobilization. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Molecular_cuisine" title="Molecular cuisine">Molecular cuisine</a> is also a user of the substance for its gelling properties, by which it becomes a delivery vehicle for flavours.
</p><p>Between 100,000 and 170,000 wet tons of <i><a href="/wiki/Macrocystis" title="Macrocystis">Macrocystis</a></i> are harvested annually in <a href="/wiki/New_Mexico" title="New Mexico">New Mexico</a> for <a href="/wiki/Alginic_acid" title="Alginic acid">alginate</a> extraction and <a href="/wiki/Abalone" title="Abalone">abalone</a> feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy_source">Energy source</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Energy source">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Algae_fuel" title="Algae fuel">Algae fuel</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_hydrogen_production" title="Biological hydrogen production">Biological hydrogen production</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biohydrogen" title="Biohydrogen">Biohydrogen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biodiesel" title="Biodiesel">Biodiesel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethanol_fuel" title="Ethanol fuel">Ethanol fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butanol_fuel" title="Butanol fuel">Butanol fuel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vegetable_oil" title="Vegetable oil">Vegetable oil</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biogas" title="Biogas">Biogas</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrothermal_Liquefaction" title="Hydrothermal Liquefaction">Hydrothermal Liquefaction</a></div>
<p>To be competitive and independent from fluctuating support from (local) policy on the long run, biofuels should equal or beat the cost level of fossil fuels. Here, algae-based fuels hold great promise,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> directly related to the potential to produce more biomass per unit area in a year than any other form of biomass. The break-even point for algae-based biofuels is estimated to occur by 2025.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fertilizer">Fertilizer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Fertilizer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Seaweed_fertiliser" title="Seaweed fertiliser">Seaweed fertiliser</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Inisheer_landscape.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="1536" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Inisheer_landscape.jpg/220px-Inisheer_landscape.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Inisheer_landscape.jpg/330px-Inisheer_landscape.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Inisheer_landscape.jpg/440px-Inisheer_landscape.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Inisheer_landscape.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Seaweed-fertilized gardens on <a href="/wiki/Inisheer" title="Inisheer">Inisheer</a></div></div></div><p>
For centuries, seaweed has been used as a fertilizer; <a href="/wiki/George_Owen_of_Henllys" title="George Owen of Henllys">George Owen of Henllys</a> writing in the 16th century referring to drift weed in <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South Wales</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p><blockquote><p>This kind of ore they often gather and lay on great heapes, where it heteth and rotteth, and will have a strong and loathsome smell; when being so rotten they cast on the land, as they do their muck, and thereof springeth good corn, especially barley ... After spring-tydes or great rigs of the sea, they fetch it in sacks on horse backes, and carie the same three, four, or five miles, and cast it on the lande, which doth very much better the ground for corn and grass.</p></blockquote>
<p>Today, algae are used by humans in many ways; for example, as <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil_conditioner" title="Soil conditioner">soil conditioners</a>, and livestock feed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Aquatic and microscopic species are cultured in clear tanks or ponds and are either harvested or used to treat effluents pumped through the ponds. <a href="/wiki/Algaculture" title="Algaculture">Algaculture</a> on a large scale is an important type of <a href="/wiki/Aquaculture" title="Aquaculture">aquaculture</a> in some places. <a href="/wiki/Maerl" title="Maerl">Maerl</a> is commonly used as a soil conditioner.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nutrition">Nutrition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Nutrition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Edible_seaweed" title="Edible seaweed">Edible seaweed</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dulse.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="200" data-file-width="200" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b7/Dulse.JPG" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dulse.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dulse, a type of edible seaweed</div></div></div>
<p>Naturally growing seaweeds are an important source of food, especially in Asia. They provide many vitamins including: A, <a href="/wiki/Thiamine" title="Thiamine">B<sub>1</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Riboflavin" title="Riboflavin">B<sub>2</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">B<sub>6</sub></a>, <a href="/wiki/Niacin" title="Niacin">niacin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">C</a>, and are rich in <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium" title="Potassium">potassium</a>, iron, <a href="/wiki/Magnesium" title="Magnesium">magnesium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Calcium" title="Calcium">calcium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> In addition, commercially cultivated microalgae, including both algae and cyanobacteria, are marketed as nutritional supplements, such as <a href="/wiki/Spirulina_(dietary_supplement)" title="Spirulina (dietary supplement)">spirulina</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Chlorella" title="Chlorella">Chlorella</a></i> and the vitamin-C supplement from <i><a href="/wiki/Dunaliella" title="Dunaliella">Dunaliella</a></i>, high in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beta-carotene" title="Beta-carotene">beta-carotene</a>.
</p><p>Algae are national foods of many nations: China consumes more than 70 species, including <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fat_choy_(vegetable)" title="Fat choy (vegetable)">fat choy</a></i>, a cyanobacterium considered a vegetable; Japan, over 20 species;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mondragon_03_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mondragon_03-81">[81]</a></sup> Ireland, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dulse" title="Dulse">dulse</a>; <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cochayuyo" title="Cochayuyo">cochayuyo</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Laver_(seaweed)" title="Laver (seaweed)">Laver</a> is used to make "laver bread" in <a href="/wiki/Wales" title="Wales">Wales</a>, where it is known as <i>bara lawr</i>; in <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gim_(Korean_food)" title="Gim (Korean food)"><i>gim</i></a>; in Japan, <i><a href="/wiki/Nori" title="Nori">nori</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aonori" title="Aonori">aonori</a></i>. It is also used along the west coast of North America from California to <a href="/wiki/British_Columbia" title="British Columbia">British Columbia</a>, in Hawaii and by the <a href="/wiki/M%C4%81ori_people" title="Māori people">Māori</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand" title="New Zealand">New Zealand</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sea_lettuce" title="Sea lettuce">Sea lettuce</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaria_esculenta" title="Alaria esculenta">badderlocks</a> are salad ingredients in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, Ireland, <a href="/wiki/Greenland" title="Greenland">Greenland</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">Iceland</a>. Algae is being considered a potential solution for world hunger problem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oils from some algae have high levels of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unsaturated_fatty_acid" title="Unsaturated fatty acid">unsaturated fatty acids</a>. For example, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parietochloris_incisa" title="Parietochloris incisa">Parietochloris incisa</a></i> is very high in <a href="/wiki/Arachidonic_acid" title="Arachidonic acid">arachidonic acid</a>, where it reaches up to 47% of the triglyceride pool.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Some varieties of algae favored by <a href="/wiki/Vegetarianism" title="Vegetarianism">vegetarianism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Veganism" title="Veganism">veganism</a> contain the long-chain, essential <a href="/wiki/Omega-3_fatty_acid" title="Omega-3 fatty acid">omega-3 fatty acids</a>, <a href="/wiki/Docosahexaenoic_acid" title="Docosahexaenoic acid">docosahexaenoic acid</a> (DHA) and <a href="/wiki/Eicosapentaenoic_acid" title="Eicosapentaenoic acid">eicosapentaenoic acid</a> (EPA). Fish oil contains the omega-3 fatty acids, but the original source is algae (microalgae in particular), which are eaten by marine life such as <a href="/wiki/Copepod" title="Copepod">copepods</a> and are passed up the food chain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Algae have emerged in recent years as a popular source of omega-3 fatty acids for vegetarians who cannot get long-chain EPA and DHA from other vegetarian sources such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flaxseed_oil" title="Flaxseed oil">flaxseed oil</a>, which only contains the short-chain <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alpha-linolenic_acid" title="Alpha-linolenic acid">alpha-linolenic acid</a> (ALA).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollution_control">Pollution control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Pollution control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul><li>Sewage can be treated with algae,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> reducing the use of large amounts of toxic chemicals that would otherwise be needed.</li>
<li>Algae can be used to capture fertilizers in runoff from farms. When subsequently harvested, the enriched algae can be used as fertilizer.</li>
<li>Aquaria and ponds can be filtered using algae, which absorb nutrients from the water in a device called an <a href="/wiki/Algae_scrubber" title="Algae scrubber">algae scrubber</a>, also known as an algae turf scrubber.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p><a href="/wiki/Agricultural_Research_Service" title="Agricultural Research Service">Agricultural Research Service</a> scientists found that 60–90% of nitrogen runoff and 70–100% of phosphorus runoff can be captured from <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Manure_effluents&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Manure effluents (page does not exist)">manure effluents</a> using a horizontal algae scrubber, also called an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Algal_turf_scrubber" title="Algal turf scrubber">algal turf scrubber</a> (ATS). Scientists developed the ATS, which consists of shallow, 100-foot raceways of nylon netting where algae colonies can form, and studied its efficacy for three years. They found that algae can readily be used to reduce the nutrient runoff from agricultural fields and increase the quality of water flowing into rivers, streams, and oceans. Researchers collected and dried the nutrient-rich algae from the ATS and studied its potential as an organic fertilizer. They found that cucumber and corn seedlings grew just as well using ATS organic fertilizer as they did with commercial fertilizers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> Algae scrubbers, using bubbling upflow or vertical waterfall versions, are now also being used to filter aquaria and ponds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Polymers">Polymers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Polymers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various polymers can be created from algae, which can be especially useful in the creation of bioplastics.  These include hybrid plastics, cellulose based plastics, poly-lactic acid, and bio-polyethylene.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>  Several companies have begun to produce algae polymers commercially, including for use in flip-flops<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> and in surf boards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bioremediation">Bioremediation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Bioremediation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The alga <i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Stichococcus_bacillaris&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Stichococcus bacillaris (page does not exist)">Stichococcus bacillaris</a></i> has been seen to colonize silicone resins used at archaeological sites; <a href="/wiki/Biodegradation" title="Biodegradation">biodegrading</a> the synthetic substance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pigments">Pigments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Pigments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The natural <a href="/wiki/Pigment" title="Pigment">pigments</a> (<a href="/wiki/Carotenoid" title="Carotenoid">carotenoids</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chlorophyll" title="Chlorophyll">chlorophylls</a>) produced by algae can be used as alternatives to chemical <a href="/wiki/Dye" title="Dye">dyes</a> and coloring agents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>
The presence of some individual algal pigments, together with specific pigment concentration ratios, are taxon-specific: analysis of their concentrations with various analytical methods, particularly <a href="/wiki/High-performance_liquid_chromatography" title="High-performance liquid chromatography">high-performance liquid chromatography</a>, can therefore offer deep insight into the taxonomic composition and relative abundance of natural algae populations in sea water samples.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stabilizing_substances">Stabilizing substances</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Stabilizing substances">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Carrageenan" title="Carrageenan">Carrageenan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chondrus_crispus" title="Chondrus crispus">Chondrus crispus</a></div>
<p>Carrageenan, from the red alga <i>Chondrus crispus</i>, is used as a stabilizer in milk products.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_images">Additional images</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Algae&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Additional images">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
